CHAPTER I



an UNDERGRADUATE AT leisure





Wilfrid Athel go down invalid a few day after the beginning of

Trinity term. the event be not unanticipated. at Christmas it have be

clear enough that he be overtax himself; his father remark on the

fact with anxiety, and urge moderation, his own peculiar virtue.

Wilfrid, whose battle with circumstance be all before he, decline to

believe that the body be anything but the very humble servant of the

will. so the body take its revenge.



he have be delicate in childhood, and the stage of hardy naturalism

which interpose itself between tender juvenility and the birth of

self-consciousness do not in his case last long enough to establish his

frame in the vigour to which it be tend. there be nothing sickly

about he; it be only an excess of nervous vitality that would not

allow body to keep pace with mind. he be a boy to be, intellectually,

hold in leash, say the doctor. but that be easy say than do.

what system of sedative could one apply to a youngster whose

imagination work he to a fever during a simple walk by the seashore,

who if book be forcibly withhold console himself with the

composition of five-act tragedy, intersperse with lyric to which he

supply original strain? Mr. Athel conceive a theory that such

exuberance of emotionality might be counterbalance by study of a

strictly positive nature; a tutor be engage to ground young Wilfrid in

mathematic and the physical science. the result be that the tutor's

enthusiasm for these pursuit communicate itself after a brief

repugnance to the versatile pupil; instinct of mastery become as vivid

in the study of Euclid and the chemical element as formerly in the

humaner path of learning; the plan have fail. in the upshot Wilfrid

be send to school; if that do not develop the animal in he, nothing

would.



he be not quite three-and-twenty when the break-down remove he from

Oxford. go to Balliol with a scholarship, he have from the first be

mark for great thing, at all event by the measure of the school.

Removal from the system of home education have in truth seem to answer

in some degree the end aim at; the lad take his fair share of cricket

and football, and keep clear of nervous crisis. at the same time he make

extraordinary progress with his book. he acquire with extreme

facility, and his ambition never allow he to find content in a second

place; conquest become his habit; he grow to deem it the order of nature

that Wilfrid Athel's name should come first in the list. hence a

reputation to support. during his early term at Balliol he fag as

hard as the mere dullard whose dear life depend upon a first class and

a subsequent tutorship. what he would make of himself in the end be

uncertain; university distinction would probably be of small moment to

he as soon as they be achieve, for already he spend the great

portion of his strength in line of study quite apart from the

curriculum, and fate have bless he with exemption from sordid care.

he lead in a set devoted to what be call advanced idea; without

flatter himself that he be on the way to solve the problem of the

universe, he have satisfaction in review the milestone which remove

he from the unconscious man, and already clutch at a measure of

positive wisdom in the suspicion that lie might shortly have to lay

aside his school-book and recommence his education under other

teacher. as yet he be whole-hearted in the pursuit of learn. the

intellectual audacity which be willnot to be the key-note of his

conversation do not, as his detractor hold, indicate mere

bumptiousness and defect of self-measurement; it be simply the florid

redundancy of a young mind which glory in its strength, and play at

victory in anticipation. it be true that he could not brook the

semblance of inferiority; if it be only five minute' chat in the

Quad, he must come off with a phrase or an epigram; so those duller

head who call Athel affect be not wholly without their

justification. those who shrug their shoulder with the remark that

he be overdo it, and would not last out to the end of the race,

enjoy a more indisputable triumph. one evening, when Athel be take

the brilliant lead in an argument on 'Fate, free-will, foreknowledge

absolute,' his brain begin to whirl, tobacco-smoke seem to have dull

all the light before his eye, and he fall from his chair in a

fainting-fit.



he need nothing but rest; that, however, be imperative. Mr. Athel

bring he to London, and the family go down at once to their house

in Surrey. Wilfrid be an only son and an only child. his father have

be a widower for nearly ten year; for the last three his house have

be direct by a widow sister, Mrs. Rossall, who have twin girl. Mr.

Athel find it no particular hardship to get away from town and pursue

his work at the Firs, a delightful house in the midst of Surrey's

fair scenery, nor would Mrs. Rossall allow that the surrender of high

season cost she any effort. this lady have just complete she

thirty-second year; her girl be in their tenth. she be comely and

know it, but a constitutional indolence have preserve she from become

a woman of fashion, and have nurture in she a reflective mood, which, if

it lead to no marked originality of thought, at all event contribute to

an appearance of culture. at the time of her husband's death she be at

the point where graceful inactivity so often degenerate into

slovenliness. Mrs. Rossall's homekeepe tendency and the grow

childhood of her twin tend to persuade she that her youth be go;

even the new spring fashion stir she to but languid interest, and

her music, in which she have some attainment, be all but lay aside.

with widowhood begin a new phase of her life. her mourning be

unaffected; it lead she to pietism; she spend her day in religious

observance, and her night in the study of the grave literature. she

would have enter the Roman Church but for her brother's interposition.

the end of this third year of discipline be bring about another

change, perhaps less obvious to herself than to those who mark she

course with interest, as several people do. her reading become less

ascetic, she pass to George Herbert and the 'Christian Year,' and by

way of the decoration of altar proceed to thought for her personal

adornment. a certain journal of society which she have long ago abandon

begin to show itself occasionally in her room, though only as yet by

oversight leave to view. she speak with her brother on the subject of

certain invitation, long neglect, and do not seem displeased when he

go beyond her own motion to propose the issuing of card for a

definite evening. then come Wilfrid's break-down. there be really no

need, say Mr. Athel, that she should transfer herself immediately to

the country, just when everybody be well settle in town. but Mrs.

Rossall prefer to go; she be not sure that the juncture have not some

connection with her own spiritual life. and she maintain, on the

whole, a seemly cheerfulness.



Mr. Athel be an egyptologist of some distinction. though not in person

or manner suggestive of romantic antecedent, he have yet come by this

taste in a way which border on romance. travel in Southern Europe

at about the age which Wilfrid have now reach, he have the good fortune

to rescue from drown an italian gentleman then on a tour in Greece.

the Italian have a fair daughter, who be travel with he, and she,

after an acquaintance of a few week, Athel demand by way of

recompense. her father be an enthusiastic student of egyptian

antiquity; the Englishman ply at one and the same time his wooing

and the study of hieroglyphic, with marked success in both direction.

the Mr. Athel who at that time represent parental authority, or at all

event claim filial deference, be anything but pleased with the step

his son have take; he be a highly respectable dealer in grain, and,

after the manner of highly respectable man of commerce, would have have

his eld son espouse some countrywoman yet more respectable. it be

his opinion that the lad have be entrap by an adventurous foreigner.

Philip Athel, who have a will of his own, wed his italian maiden,

bring she to England, and fight down prejudice. a year or two later

he be at work in Egypt, where lie remain for some twelve month; his

study progress. subsequently he publish certain paper which be

recognise as valuable. Wilfrid find the amusement of his childhood in

his father's pursuit; he begin to decipher hieratic not much later than

he learn to read English. scarab be his sacred plaything, and by

the time of his go to school he be able to write letter home in a

demotic which would not perhaps have satisfied Champollion or Brugsch,

but yet be sufficiently marvellous to his schoolfellow and gratifying

to his father.



for the rest, Philip Athel be a typical english gentleman. he enjoy

out-of-door sport as keenly as he do the pursuit of his study; he have

scarcely know a day's illness in his life, owe, he maintain, to the

wisdom with which he arrange his day. three hour of study be, he

hold, as much as any prudent man would allow himself. he be always in

excellent spirit, ever ready to be of service to a friend, live with

much moderation on victual of the good quality procurable, take his

autumnal holiday abroad in a gentlemanly manner. with something of

theoretic Radicalism in his political view, he combine a stout respect

for british social institution; affect to be above vulgar

prejudice, he be in reality much prepossess in favour of hereditary

position, and as time go on do occasionally half wish that the love

he have bestow on his italian wife have be give to some english lady

of 'good' family. he be liberal, frank, amiably autocratic in his home,

apt to be peppery with inferior who miss the line of perfect respect,

candid and reasonable with equal or superior. for his boy he reserve

a store of manly affection, seldom express itself save in bluff

fashion; his sister he patronise with much kindness, though he despise

her judgment. one have now and then a feeling that his material

circumstance aid greatly in make he the genial man he be, that

with beef and claret of inferior quality he might not have be

altogether so easy to get along with. but that again be an illustration

of the english character.



we find the family assemble for breakfast at the Firs one delightful

morning at the end of July. the window of the room be throw open,

and there stream in with the sunlight fresh and delicious odour,

tonic alike of mind and body. from the Scotch fir whence the dwelling

take its name come a scent which mingle with waft breath from the

remoter heather, and the creeper about the house-front, the lovely

bloom and leafage skirt the lawn, contribute to the atmosphere of

health and joy. it be nine o'clock. the urn be on the gleam table,

the bell be sound, Mr. Athel step in straight from the lawn,

fresh after his ten minute' walk about the garden. Wilfrid Athel

appear at the same moment; he be dark-complexioned and have black,

glossy hair; his cheek be hollower than they should have be, but he

have not the aspect of an invalid. Mrs. Rossall glide into the room

behind he, fresh, fair, undemonstrative. then come the twin, by name

Patty and Minnie, delicate, with promise of their mother's english style

of beauty; it be very hard to distinguish they, their uncle have

honestly give up the pretence long ago, and occasionally remonstrate

with his sister on the absurdity of dress they exactly alike. the

last to enter the room be the governess, Miss Emily Hood.



Mr. Athel, having pronounce a grace, mention that he think of

run up to town; do anybody wish to give he a commission? Mrs.

Rossall look thoughtful, and say she would make a note of two or

three thing.



'I haven't much faith in that porridge regimen, Wilf,' remark the

master of the house, as he help himself to chicken and tongue. 'we be

not highlander. it's dangerous to make diet too much a matter of

theory. your example be infectious; first the twin; now Miss Hood.

Edith, do you propose to become a pervert to porridge?'



'I have no taste for it,' reply his sister, who have become

absent-minded.



'there's a certain dishonesty about it, moreover,' Mr. Athel pursue.

'porridge should be eat with salt. milk _and_ sugar--didn't I hear a

suggestion of golden syrup, more honestly call treacle, yesterday?

these thing constitute evasion, self-deception at the least. in your

case, Miss Hood, the regimen be clearly fruitful of ill result.'



'of what kind, Mr. Athel?'



'obviously it lead to diminution of appetite. you be in the habit of

eat a satisfactory breakfast; at present some two ounce of that

farinaceous mess--'



'my dear Philip!' interpose Mrs. Rossall, still absently.



I hold that I be within my right,' assert her brother. 'if Miss Hood

go down into Yorkshire in a state of emaciation--'



Wilfrid and the twin show amusement.



'to begin with,' pursue Mr. Athel, 'I hold that sweet food the first

thing in the morning be a mistake; the appetite be check in an

artificial way, and impair. even coffee--'



'you would recommend a return to flagon of ale?' suggest Wilfrid.



'I be not sure that it ben't well dietetically.'



Mrs. Rossall have take an egg, but, after fruitlessly chip at the

shell throughout this conversation, put down her spoon and appear to

abandon the effort to commence her meal. presently she break silence,

speak with some diffidence.



'I really think I will go to town with you, Philip,' she say. 'I want

some thing you cann't very well get I, and then I ought to go and see

the redwing. I might persuade Beatrice to come to we for a day or two.'



'do so by all mean. you're quite sure,' he add with a smile, 'that I

couldn't save you the trouble of the journey? I have no objection to

visit the redwing.'



'I think it will be well if I go myself,' reply Mrs. Rossall, with a

far-off look. 'I might call on one or two other people.'



having decide this point, she find herself able to crack the egg. the

anticipation of her day in London make she quite gay throughout the

meal.



the carriage be at the door by ten o'clock, to drive to dealing, the

near station, some four mile away. the twin have go upstairs with

Miss Hood to their lesson, and Wilfrid be saunter about the hall.

his father pause by he on the way to the carriage.



'what do you propose to do with yourself, Wilf?' he ask.



'Ride, I think.'



'do. go over to hilstead and lunch there. capital lunch they give you at

the inn; the last time I be there they cook I one of the good chop

I ever eat. Oberon want exercise; make a day of it.'



'very well.'



'you're not look quite so well, I'm afraid,' remark his father,

with genuine solicitude in his tone. 'haven't be read, have you?'



'no.'



'no imprudence, mind. I must stop that porridge regimen; it don't

suit you. ready, Edith?' he shout heartily at the foot of the stair.



Mrs. Rossall come down, button her glove.



'if I be you, Wilf,' she say, 'I'd go off somewhere for the day. the

twin will only worry you.'



Wilfrid laugh.



'I be go to eat unexampled chop at the "Waggoner" in hilstead,' he

reply.



'that's right. good-bye, my dear boy. I wish you'd get fat.'



'Pooh, I'm all right.'



the landau roll away. Wilfrid still loiter in the hall, a singular

look of doubt on his face. in a room above one of the twin be have a

music lesson; a certain finger-exercise be be drum with

persistent endeavour at accuracy.



'how can she bear that morning after morning?' the young man murmur to

himself.



he take his straw hat and go round to the stable. Oberon be be

groom. Wilfrid pat the horse's sleek neck, and talk a little with

the man. at length he make up his mind to go and prepare for riding;

Oberon would be ready for he in a few minute.



in the porch Patty run to meet he.



'Truant!' Wilfrid exclaim. 'have I catch you in the act of escape?'



'I be go to look for you,' say the child, put her arm through

his and swinge upon he. 'we want to know if you'll be back for

lunch.'



'who want to know?'



'I and Minnie and Miss Hood.'



'oh, you be Patty, then, be you?'



this be an old form of joke. the child shake her dark curl with a

half-annoyed gesture, but still swing on her cousin as he move into the

house. Wilfrid pass his arm about she playfully.



'cann't you make up your mind, Wilf?' she ask.



'oh yes, my mind be quite make up,' he reply, with a laugh.



'and willn't you tell I?'



'tell you? ah, about lunch. no, I shall not be back.'



'you willn't? oh, I be sorry.'



'why be you sorry, indistinguishable little maiden?' he ask, draw

out one of her curl between his finger, and let it spring back

again into its circling beauty.



'we think it would be so nice, we four at lunch.'



'I be warn to avoid you. the tone of conversation would try my weak

head; I be not capable yet of intellectual effort.'



the little girl look at he with puzzled eye.



'well, it cann't be help,' she say. 'I must go back to my lesson.'



she run off, and Wilfrid go up to his dressing-room. when he come

down, Oberon be paw the gravel before the door. he mount and ride

away.



his spirit, which at first seem to suffer some depression, take

vigour once more from the air of the down. he put Oberon at a leap or

two, then let the breeze sing in his ear as he be bear at a gallop

over the summer land, golden with sunlight. in spite of his still worn

look, health be manifest in the upright vigour of his form, and in his

eye gleam the untroubled joy of existence. Hope just now be strong

within he, a hope define and point to an end attainable; he know

that henceforth the many bounding and voiceful stream of his life would

unite in one strong flow onward to a region of orient glory which shine

before he as the bourne hitherto but dimly imagine. on, Oberon, on! no

speed that would not lag behind the fore-flight of a heart's desire. let

the stretch of green-shadow woodland sweep by like a dream; let the

fair, sweet meadow-side smile for a moment and vanish; let the dark

hill-summit rise and sink. it be the time of youth and hope, of

boundless faith in the world's promise, of breathless pursuit.



Hilstead be gain long before lunch could be think of. Wilfrid ride

on, and circle back towards the hostelry famous for chop about the

hour of noon. he put up his horse, and stray about the village till

his meal be ready; after he have eat it he smoke a cigar among

hollyhock and sunflower. then impatience possess he. he look at

his watch several time, annoy to find that so little of the day be

spend. when he at last set forth again, it be to ride at walk pace

in the direction of home. he reach a junction of road, and wait

there for several minute, unable to decide upon his course. he end by

throw the rein on Oberon's neck.



'go which way you will,' he say aloud.



Oberon pace forward to the homeward route.



'so be it. on, then! an hour will bring we to the Firs.'



the house be all but reach, when Wilfrid catch a glimpse of a straw

hat move into a heath-clothe hollow a hundred yard from the road. he

press on. at the gate stand a gardener.



'James,' he cry, leap down, 'take the horse to the stable, will

you?'



and, instead of go up to the house, he walk back in the direction

he have come till he reach the hollow in which the straw hat have

disappear. Miss Hood sit on the ground, read. she be about to

rise, but Wilfrid beg she not to move, and throw himself into a

reclining posture.



'I see you as I ride past,' he say, in a friendly way. 'I suppose the

twin be stray?'



'they be at Greenhaws,' be the reply, 'Mrs. Winter call for they

immediately after lunch. she will bring they back early in the evening.'



'ah!'



he pluck sprig of heather. Miss Hood turn to her book.



'I've have a magnificent ride,' Wilfrid begin again. 'surely there be no

country in England so glorious as this. don't you enjoy it?'



'very much.'



'I have never see the Yorkshire moor. the scenery, of course, be of a

much wilder kind?'



'I have not see they myself,' say the governess.



'I think you might have take your holiday sometimes in that

direction.'



'no. we use to go to a seaside place in Lincolnshire call

Cleethorpes. I suppose you never hear of it?'



'I think not.'



Wilfrid continue to pluck heather, and let his eye catch a glimpse of

her face now and then. Miss Hood be a year young than himself, and

have well outgrow girlishness. she be of very slight build, look

indeed rather frail; but her face, though lack colour, have the

firmness of health. it be very broad at the forehead, and taper down

into narrowness; the eye seem set at an unusual distance from each

other, though the nose be thin and of perfect form, its profile make

but a slight angle away from the line of the brow. her lip be large,

but finely curve; the chin be prominent, the throat long. she have warm

brown hair.



few would at first sight have call her face beautiful, but none could

deny the beauty of her hand. unglove at present, they lie on the open

page of the book, unsurpassable for delicate loveliness. when he do

not venture to look high, Wilfrid let his eye feed on the turn of the

wrist, the faint blue line and sinuous muscle, the pencilling about

the finger-joint, the delicate white and pink nail.



Miss Hood be habitually silent when in the company of other than the

child. when she reply to a question it be without timidity, but in

few, well-choose word. yet her manner do not lack cheerfulness; she

impress no one as be unhappy, and alone with the twin she be

often gay enough. she be self-possess, and have the manner of a lady,

though in her position this be rather to be observe in what she

refrain from do than in what she do. Wilfrid have, on first meeting

she, remark to himself that it must imply a certain force of

individuality to vary so distinctly from the commonplace even under the

disadvantage of complete self-suppression; he have now come to understand

well the way in which that individuality betray itself.



'shall you go to Cleethorpes this year?' be his next question.



'I think not. I shall most likely pass the holiday at home.'



'and study electricity?'



in a former conversation she have surprise he by some unexpected

knowledge of the principle of electricity, and explain the

acquirement by tell he that this subject be her father's favourite

study. Wilfrid put the question now with a smile.



'yes, very likely,' she reply, smile also, but faintly. 'it give my

father pleasure when I do so.'



'you have not a keen interest in the subject yourself?'



'I try to have.'



her voice be of singular quality; if she raise it the effect be not

agreeable, owe possibly to its lack of strength, but in low tone,

such as she employ at present, it fall on the ear with a peculiar

sweetness, a natural melody in its modulation.



'the way in which you speak of your father interest I,' say Wilfrid,

lean his chin upon his hand, and gaze at she freely. 'you seem so

united with he in sympathy.'



she do not turn her eye to he, but her face gather brightness.



'in sympathy, yes,' she reply, speak now with more readiness. 'our

taste often differ, but we be always at one in feeling. we have be

companion ever since I can remember.'



'be your mother live?'



'yes.'



something in the tone of the brief affirmative keep Wilfrid from far

questioning.



'I wonder,' he say, 'what you think of the relation exist between

myself and my father. we be excellent friend, don't you think?

strange--one don't think much about such thing till some occasion

bring they forward. whether there be deep sympathy between we, I

couldn't say. certainly there be many subject on which I should not

dream of speak to he unless necessity arise; partly, I suppose, that

be male reserve, and partly english reserve. if novel be to be

trust, french parent and child speak together with much more

freedom; on the whole that must be well.'



she make no remark.



'my father,' he continue, 'be eminently a man of sense; if I reflect on

my boyhood, I see how admirable his treatment of I have always be. I

fancy I must have be at one time rather hard to manage; I know I be

very passionate and stubbornly self-willed. yet he neither let I have

my own way nor anger I by his opposition. in fact, he make I respect

he. now that we stand on equal term, I dare say he have something of

the same feeling towards myself. and so it come that we be excellent

friend.'



she listen with a scarcely perceptible smile.



'perhaps this seem to you a curiously dispassionate way of treat

such a subject,' Wilfrid add, with a laugh. 'it illustrate what I

mean in say I doubt whether there be deep sympathy between we.

your own feeling for your father be clearly one of devotedness. you

would think no sacrifice of your own wish too great if he ask it of

you.'



'I cannot imagine any sacrifice, which my father could ask, that I

should refuse.'



she speak with some difficulty, as if she wish to escape the subject.



'perhaps that be a virtue that your sex help to explain,' say Wilfrid,

musingly.



'you do not know,' he add, when a bee have hum between they for half

a minute, 'how constant my regret be that my mother do not live till I

be old enough to make a friend of she. you know that she be an

italian? there be a sympathy take out of my life. I believe I have

more of the italian nature than the English, and I know my mother's

presence would be priceless to I now that I could talk with she. what

unsatisfactory creature we be as child, so imperfect, so deficient!

it be bad with boy than with girl. Compare, for instance, the twin

with boy of ten. what coarse, awkward, unruly lump of boisterousness

youngster mostly be at that age! I dislike boy, and more than ever

when I remember myself at that stage. what an insensible, ungrateful,

brainless, and heartless brat I be!'



'you must be wrong in one respect,' she return, watch a large

butterfly. 'you could not have be brainless.'



'oh, the foundation of tolerable wit be there, no doubt; but it be

just that undeveloped state that irritate I. suppose I be now ten

year old, and that glorious butterfly before I; should I not leap at

it and stick a pin through it--young savage? precisely what a Hottentot

boy would do, except that he would be free from the apish folly of

pretend a scientific interest, not really exist. I rejoice to have

live out of my boyhood; I would not go through it again for anything

short of a thousand year of subsequent maturity.'



she just glance at he, a light of laughter in her eye. she be

abandon herself to the pleasure of hear he speak.



'that picture of my mother,' he pursue, drop his voice again, 'do

not do her justice. even at twelve year old--(she die when I be

twelve)--I could not help see and know how beautiful she be. I

have think of she of late more than I ever do; sometimes I suffer a

passion of grief that one so beautiful and lovable have go and leave a

mere dumb picture. I suppose even my memory of she will grow fainter and

fainter, found as it be on imperfect understanding, dim appreciation.

she use to read Italian to I--first the italian, then the English--and

I think it, as often as not, a bore to have to listen to she!

thank Heaven, I have the book she use, and can now go over the piece,

and try to recall her voice.'



the butterfly be go, but the bee still hum about they. the hot

afternoon air be unstirred by any breeze.



'how glad I be,' Wilfrid exclaim when he have brood for a few

moment, 'that I happen to see you as I ride past! I should have

wander restlessly about the house in vain, seek for some one to

talk to. and you listen so patiently. it be pleasant to be here and talk

so freely of thing I have always have to keep in my own mind. look, do

look at that bastion of cloud over the sycamore! what glorious gradation

of tint! what a snowy crown!'



'that be a pretty spray,' he add, hold to her one that he have

pluck.



she look at it; then, as he still hold it out, take it from he. the

exquisite finger touch his own redder and coarser one.



'have you friend in Dunfield?' he ask.



'friend?'



'any real friend, I mean--any girl who give you real companionship?'



'scarcely that.'



'how shall you spend your time when you be not deep in electric? what

do you mean to read these holiday?'



'chiefly german, I think. I have only just begin to read it.'



'and I cann't read it at all. now and then I make a shot at the meaning

of a note in a german edition of some classical author, every time

weaken as one grow in year; perhaps the sphere of one's keener

'there be precisely the source of dissatisfaction. my whole time, and

one could linger for ever. Athens at her fairest, Rome at her

and when be one to live? I cannot lose myself in other man's activity

and enjoyment. I must have a life of my own, outside the wall of a

library. it would be easy to give up all ambition of knowledge, to

forget all the joy and sorrow that have be and pass into nothingness;

to know only the eternity of a present hour. might one not learn more in

one instant of unreflecte happiness than by toil on to a mummie

he again raise his eye to her face. it be fix in a cold, absent

more of her inner self than he have yet divine.



'what be your thought?' he ask quietly.



she smile, recover her wonte passiveness.



'have you not often much the same trouble?'



'they arc only for the mind which be strong enough to meet and overcome

'but look, my mind have give way already! I be imbecile. for ever I

shall be on the point of a break-down, and each successive one will

'I should think,' she say gravely, 'that you suggest a truth. very

'ah! and that?'



'it will not enlighten you, for it be only the choice of a natural and

pure literature, poetry in its wide sense; that be the wise choice.

think; we feed ourselves with the secondhand wisdom of paltry

she shake her head.

the elbow on which he rest move a few inch near to she. he see

'no, it be not needless; and even now you speak in a way which trouble

'for all that, you speak of the recovery of my health as if I be still

Miss Hood rise to her foot in that graceful effortless way of which

'you attribute a meaning to my word that I never think of,' she say,

Wilfrid also rise.



'and you give I credit for understand myself, for be as much

'surely I do, Mr. Athel.'



'you be go to the house? it be nearly five o'clock; your conscience

he hesitate before move away.



'how little that cloud have change its form! I should like to stay here

'no, there we have only mill-smoke.'



she smile, and pass from the hollow to the road.









CHAPTER II



BEATRICE redwing





midway in breakfast next morning, at a moment when Mrs. Rossall be

while she sleep, let we learn a little more of her history. some

five-and-twenty year previously, Alfred Redwing be a lecturer on Greek

and Latin at a small college in the North of England, make shift to

Poor Redwing conceive a suspicion that his wife desire to murder he;

restraint, and never again know freedom. in less than a year he die, a

moping maniac.



Mrs. Redwing be an invalid thenceforth; probably it be only the

existence of her child that save her life. an affection of the heart in

course of time declare itself, but, though her existence be believe

inability to outgrow early condition.



Beatrice inherit her mother's mental restriction, but be endow

ready to chaperon Beatrice Redwing. once in the world from which thought

be banish, she seem as thoughtless as any. her spiritual conviction

the child reply that they be.



'where be Miss Hood?'

'why, Philip, of course I do.'

CHAPTER III



LYRICAL





Miss Hood do not, of course, dine with the family. though, as Mrs.

Rossall say, it be a distinct advantage to have in the house a

governess whom one could in many respect treat as an equal, yet there

be naturally a limit, in this as in all other matter. we have not yet,

either in fact or in sentiment, quite outgrow the social stage in which

personal hire set on the hire a stigma of servitude. Mrs. Rossall

be not unaware that, in all that concerned intellectual refinement, her

governess be considerably superior to herself, and in personal

refinement not less a lady; but the fact of quarterly payment, spite of

all this, inevitably indicate a place below the salt. Mr. Athel,

though, as we have see, anxious to indulge himself in humane regard

whenever social regulation permit, be the last man to suffer in his

household serious innovation upon traditional propriety.



so Miss Hood--Emily, as she be call by the little group of people

with the child. after that the evening be her own, save that the

Wilfrid be at Balliol when Miss Hood first arrive; he see she for the

CHAPTER IV



a conflict of opinion





Beatrice Redwing's visit only extend over the second day, and during

that there be little, if any, separate conversation between her and

CHAPTER V



the SHADOW of HOME





the house which be the end of Emily's journey be situate two mile

outside the town of Dunfield, on the high road go southward, just

before it enter upon a rise tract of common land know as the heath.

it be one of a row of two-storied dwelling, build of glaze brick,

each with a wide project window on the right hand of the front door,

and with a patch of garden rail in from the road, the row be part

of a straggling colony which be call Banbrigg. immediately opposite

these house stand an ecclesiastical edifice of depress appearance,

stone-build, wholly without ornament, present a corner to the

highway, a chapel-of-ease for worshipper unable to go as far as

Dunfield in the one direction or the village of Pendal in the other.

scatter about be dwelling-house old and new; the former being

cottage of the poor and dirty kind, the latter brick structure

of the most unsightly form, evidently aim at constitute themselves

into a thoroughfare, and, in point of fact, already rejoice in the

name of Regent Street. there be a public-house, or rather, as it

frankly style itself in large letter on the window, a dram-shop; and

there be two or three place for the sale of very miscellaneous

article, exhibit the same specimen of discourage stock throughout

the year. at no season, and under no advantage of sky, be Banbrigg a

delectable abode. though within easy reach of country which be not

without rural aspect, it be mark too unmistakably with the squalor

of a manufacture district. its existence impress one as casual; it

be a mere bit of Dunfield get away from the main mass, and having

bring its dirt with it. the stretch of road between it and the bridge

by which the river be cross into Dunfield have in its long, hard

ugliness something dispirit. though hedge border it here and

there, they be stunt and grime; though field be see on this

side and on that, the grass have absorb too much mill-smoke to exhibit

wholesome verdure; it be feed upon by sheep and cow, seemingly turn

in to be out of the way till need for slaughter, and by the sorriest

of superannuated horse. the land be blight by the curse of what we

name--use a word as ugly as the thing it represent--industrialism.



as the cab bring she along this road from Dunfield station, emily

thought of the down, the woodland, the fair pasture of Surrey. there

be sorrow at her heart, even a vague torment fear. it would be hard

to find solace in Banbrigg.



Hither her parent have come to live when she be thirteen year old, she

home having previously be in another and a large manufacturing town.

her father be a man mark for ill-fortune: it pursue he from his

entrance into the world, and would inevitably--you read it in his

face--hunt he into a sad grave. he be the young of a large family;

his very birth have be an add misery to a household struggle with

want. his education be of the slight; at twelve year of age he be

already support himself, or, one would say, keep himself above the

point of starvation; and at three-and-twenty--the age when Wilfrid Athel

be enter upon life in the joy of freedom--be ludicrously bankrupt, a

petty business he have establish be sell up for a debt something

short of as many pound as he have year. he drift into indefinite

mercantile clerkship, an existence possibly preferable to that of the

fourth circle of Inferno, and then seem at length to have fall upon

a piece of good luck, such as, accord to a maxim of pathetic optimism

wherewith he be willnot to cheer himself, must come to every man soon or

later--provide he do not die of hunger whilst it be on the way. he

marry a schoolmistress, one Miss Martin, who be responsible for the

teaching of some twelve or fifteen child of tender age, and who, what

be more, own the house in which she keep school. the result be that

James Hood once more establish himself in business, or rather in

several business, vague, indescribable, save by those who be unhappy

enough to understand such matter--a commission agency, a life insurance

agency and a fire insurance ditto, I know not what. yet the semblance of

prosperity be fleeting. as if connection with he mean failure, his

wife's school, which she have not abandon (let we employ negative term

in speak of this pair), begin to fall off; ultimately no school be

left. it do in truth appear that Miss Martin have suffer something in

become Mrs. Hood. at her marriage she be five-and-twenty, fairly

good-look, in temper a trifle exigent perhaps, sanguine, and capable

of exertion; she could not claim more than superficial instruction, but

teach reading and write with the usual success which attend teacher

of these element. after the birth of her first child, Emily, her moral

nature show an unaccountable weakening; the origin be no doubt

physical, but in story-telling we dwell very much on the surface of

thing; it be not permit we to describe human nature too accurately.

the exigence of her temper become something generally describe by a

harsh term; she lose her interest in the work which she have

unwillingly entrust for a time to an assistant; she find the

condition of her life hard. alas, they grow hard. after Emily, two

child be successively bear; fate be kind to they, and neither

survive infancy. their mother fall into fret, into hysteria; some

change in her life seem imperative, and at length she persuade she

husband to quit the town in which they live, and begin life anew

elsewhere. begin life anew! James Hood be forty year old; he

possess, as the net result of his commercial enterprise, a capital of

a hundred and thirty pound. the house, of course, could be let, and

would bring five-and-twenty pound a year. this it be resolve to do.

he have have certain dealing in Dunfield, and in Dunfield he would strike

his tent--that be to say, in Banbrigg, whence he walk daily to a

little office in the town. rent be low in Banbrigg, and it be

beyond the range of certain municipal taxing.



Mrs. Hood possess still her somewhat genteel furniture. one article

be a piano, and upon this she teach Emily her note. it have be a

fairly good piano once, but the key have become very loose. they be

loose than ever, now that Emily try to play on they, on her return

from Surrey.



Business do not thrive in Dunfield; yet there be more than ever need

that it should, for to neglect Emily's education would be to deal

her battle with the world. poor Emily a feeble, overgrown child, need

fresh air, which she could not get, need food of a well kind, just

as unattainable. large-eye, thin-check Emily; she, too, already in

the clutch of the great brute world, the helpless victim of a

civilisation which make its food of those the heart most pity. how

well if her last sigh have be draw in infancy, if she have lain with

the little brother and sister in that gaunt, grimy cemetery, under the

shadow of mill chimney! she be reserve for other grief; for

consolation, it be true, but--



Education she do get, by hook or by crook; there be dire pinching to

pay for it, and, too well know this, the child strive her utmost to

use the opportunity offer she. each morning go into Dunfield,

take with she some sandwich that be call dinner, walk home

again by tea-time, tired, hungry--ah, hungry no matter the weather, she

must walk her couple of mile--it be at least so far to the school. in

winter you see she set forth with her waterproof and umbrella, the

too-heavy bag of book on her arm; sometimes the wind and rain beating

as if to delay she--they, too, cruel. in summer the hot day try she

perhaps still more; she reach home in the afternoon well-nigh

she? so gentle she be, so dreadfully shy and timid, her eye so eager,

so full of unconscious pathos. 'Hood's little girl,' say the people on

the way who see her pass daily, and, however completely stranger, they

say it with a certain kindness of tone and meaning. a little thing that

happen one day--take it as an anecdote. on her way to school she

pass some boy who be pelt a most wretched dog, a poor, scraggy

beast drive into a corner. emily, so timid usually she could not raise

her eye before a stranger, stop, quiver all over, _command_

they to cease their brutality, divine compassion become a heroism. the

boy somehow do her bidding, and walk on together. Emily stay

behind, open her bag, throw something for the dog to eat. it be half

her dinner.



her mind brace itself. she have a passionate love of learn; all book

be food to she. fortunately there be the library of the Mechanics'

Institute; but for that she would have come short of mental sustenance,

for her father have never be able to buy mole than a dozen volume, and

these all deal with matter of physical science. the strange thing she

dust upon they; history of Greece and Rome ('not much ask for,

Mosheim's 'ecclesiastical history,' work of Swedenborg, all the poetry

she could lay hand on, novel not a few. one day she ask for a book

on 'Gymnoblastic Hydroids'; the amazing title in the catalogue have

fill she with curiosity; she must know the meaning of everything. she

be not idle, Emily.



but thing in the home be go from bad to bad. when Emily be

sixteen, her father scarcely know where to look for each day's dinner.

something must be do. activity take a twofold direction. first of all,

Emily get work as a teacher in an infant's school. it be at her own

must take some step. she earn a mere trifle; but it be earn,

instead of be a source of expense. and in the meantime she work on

a place in the counting-house of a worst-mill, under the firm of

Dagworthy and Son. his salary be small, but the blessing of it be its

certainty; the precariousness of his existence have all but drive poor

Hood mad. there come a season of calm. Emily's sphere of work extend

itself; the school only take her morning, and for the afternoon there

Baxendales be well-to-do people; the father be, just then, mayor of

Dunfield, the mother be relate to the member of Parliament for the

town. we have have mention of they as connection of Beatrice Redwing.



at nineteen she for the first time leave home. through the baxendale she

obtain the position of governess in a family reside in Liverpool,

and remain with they till she go to London, to the Athels. these

three year in Liverpool be momentous for she; they lead she from

the house of a prosperous ship-owner, a Lancashire man, outwardly a

bluster good-temper animal, yet with an inner light which show

to Emily the constant proximity of these picture be a priceless

free command of book; here begin her acquaintance with the most modern

modify themselves, that they should do so be the note of her

progress. her prayer be for 'beauty in the inward soul,' which, if it

grow to be her conviction, be greatly--perhaps wholly--dependent on

the perception of external beauty. the development of beauty in the soul

would mean a life of ideal purity; all her instinct point to such a

life; her passionate motive converge on the one end of spiritual

chastity.



one ever-present fear she have to strive with in her progress toward

serene conviction. the misery of her parent' home haunt she, and by

no effort could she expel the superstition that she have only escape

from that for a time, that its claw would surely overtake she and fix

or think she do, a lack of independent vigour; it seem as if she

be too reliant on external circumstance; she dread what might

follow if their assistance be withdraw. to be sure she have hold she

course through the countless discouragement of early year; but that,

in look back, seem no assurance for the future; her courage, it

appear to she, have be of the unconscious kind, and might fail she

when she consciously demand it. as a child she have once walk in her

sleep, have go forth from the house, and have, before she be awaken,

cross the narrow footing of a canal-lock, a thing her nervousness

would not allow she to do at other time. this become to she a figure.

the feat she have perform when mere vital instinct guide she, she

would have fail in when attempt it with the full understanding of

its danger. suppose something happen which put an end to her

independence--failure of health, some supreme calamity at home--could

she hold on in the way of salvation? be she capable of conscious

heroism? could her soul retain its ideal of beauty if environ by

ugliness?

in the relation these thing have their humorous side; Emily may be

always dwelt in conversation on the most wretched feature of her life,

with despairing forecast. poor woman, there be little of a brighter

kind to occupy her thought. two occasion of grave anxiety be at

present trouble she, and, though he speak of they less, her husband in

no less a degree. it have just be announce to they that at the ensue

who have for year occupy the house which they own in the town of

Barnhill have give notice of departure. there be a certain

grotesqueness in the fact of James Hood be a proprietor of real

estate. twice an attempt have be make to sell the house in question,

but no purchaser could be find; the building be in poor repair, be

constantly entail expense to the landlord, and, in the event of its

become unoccupied, would doubtless wait long for another tenant. this

and to sell, even if it could be do, mean a serious loss of income.



'what do it mean, do you think?' Mrs. Hood ask, recur to the

subject of Dagworthy and his astonishing behaviour. she put the question

to a more cheerful frame of mind.



Hood scarcely dare to utter the word which come into his mind.



'you remember that they meet at the Baxendales'--'



'how do Emily behave?' the mother next inquire.



'she be very quiet. I don't think she like it. we must bear in mind

the kind of society she be use to. Young Dagworthy willn't seem of much

'but he have have a good education, haven't he?'



'pretty good, I suppose. he confess to we, though, that he couldn't

pronounce french word.'



'it's quite certain,' say Mrs. Hood, 'he wouldn't have invite you in

if you have be alone.'



'certain enough,' be the reply, in a tone wholly disintereste. 'but it

must have be just a fancy, a whim. thing of that kind don't happen

nowadays.'



'not to we, at all event,' murmur the other dejectedly.



'well, there must come what will,' she add, lean her head back once

Alas, these two sit together in the dull little room, speak in

disjoint phrase of despondency, exchange no look, no word of mutual

kindness, have they not once love each other, with the love of youth and

hope? have it not once be enough to sit through long evening and catch

with eagerness each other's light word? Time have rob they of

youth, and the injustice of the world's order have starve love to less

than a shadow of itself, to a more habit of common suffering. tender

memory be bury in the grave of child whom the resource of ever

so modest a fortune would have keep alive; the present be a mere

struggle to support existence, choke the impulse of affection. one

would not murmur at the kindly order of life, whereby passion give

place to gentle habitude, and the fiery soul of youth tame itself to

comely gravity; but that love and joy, the delight of eager sense and

of hallowed aspiration, should be smother in the foul dust of a brute

combat for bread, that the stint energy of early year should change

themselves to the blasted hope of fail manhood in a world make ill

by human perverseness, this be not easily--it may be, not well--bear

with patience. put money in thy purse; and again, put money in thy

purse; for, as the world be order, to lack current coin be to lack the

privilege of humanity, and indigence be the death of the soul.









CHAPTER VI



a visitor BY EXPRESS

'she might, if she could live apart from her mother and sister for a

it be Mr. Hood's habit to spend his evening in a little room at the

'the last daylight in the sky be pleasant to look at.'

CHAPTER VII



on the level





not the least of many mystery in the natural history of the

Cartwrights be, how they all manage to bestow themselves in the house

wonder be it do?

not by Emily, however. it cost a good deal of firmness, for the

CHAPTER VIII



a sterner wooing

its pathos, moreover. look at he casually from the outside, one

Emily could not start at once homeward. when it be certain that

CHAPTER IX



CIRCUMSTANCE





Dagworthy be absent not quite a fortnight, and he return look

anything but the well for his holiday. the wholesome colour of his

look at he with surprise and ask what illness he have be

suffering. at the mill, they do not welcome his re-appearance; his

witness his prosecution for assault and battery. at home, the servant

'let he dom I, if he loike,' she go away exclaim; 'ah'm ovver

auld to care much abaht such fond tantrum; but when he get agaate o'

dommin his awn barn, it fair maak my tooth dither ageean. the lad's aht

on his 'eead.'



that be seven o'clock in the evening. he dine an hour later, and when

in and out, and Mrs. Jenkins, who be keep up by her fear that 't'

'Mr. Richard!' she say softly. 'Mr. Richard!'



he look up. 'well? what be it?'



'yo' scahr'd I; ah thowt summat 'ad come to yo'. what's wrong wi' yo',

Mr. Richard? you look as if you could hardly he'd your heead up.'



to her surprise he speak quite calmly.



'yes, I've get a bit of a headache. get I some hot water, will you?

I'll have some brandy and go to bed.'



she begin to advise other remedy, but Dagworthy speedily check she.

do up at this hour?'



he go to business at the usual time next morning, and it seem as if

the bad have blow over; at home he be sullen, but not violent.



the third day after his return, on enter his office at the mill, he

Hood explain that he be under the necessity of search through the

'if not, you're to takee this notee to Brother Andrew

it be a journey of ten mile, lie at first over green field, with a

'Theer, lad!' one of they cry. 'Tha's doon it nah! Tha'll a' to buy

Hood be a shy man; it be misery to have attention draw to himself as

'can you change I a ten-pound note?' be his first word to the

the salesman hesitate.



'you want a silk hat?'



'yes, but not an expensive one.'



a pen be bring, and Hood be request to endorse the note. what

security--under the circumstance--such a proceeding could give, the

he stop in the middle of the pavement as if a shot have strike he.

Supposing Dagworthy have no recollection of a ten-pound note having be

some pretence.



and he feel that, in spite of absolute sincerity, he could not stand

I be represent the poor fellow's thought and feeling. whether or

CHAPTER X



at the sword'S POINT





Dagworthy in these day could scarcely be deem a man, with humanity's

the gulf between purpose and execution once pass, he have become cruel;

'believe it or not, as you choose. all I have to say be that your father

she hear, and there come into her mind the story of Cheeseman's debt.

'do you think I be lie to you for my own purpose?'

he, by now, have attain a self-control which would not desert he. so

CHAPTER XI



EMILY'S decision





Emily reach home a few minute before dinner-time. her mother come to

she from the back of the house, where thing be in Saturday tumult,

speak with a voice of fretful satisfaction.



'I'd just give you up, and be wonder whether to let the meat spoil

or begin dinner alone.'



'I be sorry to be late, mother.'



'no, you're not late, my dear,' the mother admit. 'it's only that

you're a little uncertain, and when one o'clock draw on I can never be

quite sure of you, if you're out. I must say I like punctuality, though

I dare say it's an old-fashioned kind of thing. which would you like,

potato bake or boil? I've get both, as I always think the baked

keep well for your father.'



'whichever you have yourself, mother.'



'now, child, do make a choice! as if you couldn't say which you would

prefer.'



'boil.'



'there now, you say that because you think there willn't be enough of the

other. I know very well yen always like the baked, when I have they.

don't you, now, Emily?'



'Mother, which you like! what _do_ it matter?'



'well, my dear, I'm sure I only want to please you,' say Mrs. Hood,

in her tone of patience under injury. 'I cann't see why you should be

angry with I. if I could give you more choice I would. no doubt you're

use to have potato do in all sort of superior way, but

unfortunately I ben't bring up as a cook--'



the strange look with which Emily be regard she bring she to a

pause; her voice drop.



'mother dear,' say the girl, in a low and shake tone, 'I be neither

foolish nor unkind; do try to believe that. something be trouble I.

to-day let your choice be mine.'



Mrs. Hood move away, and serve the dinner in silence.



'what be your trouble, my dear?' she ask presently. 'cann't you tell

I?'



Emily shake her head. her mother relapse into thoughtfulness, and they

finish their meal with little conversation. Mrs. Heed be just rise

from the table, when there be a sound of some one open the gate

before the house; she look to the window, and at once utter an

exclamation of astonishment.



'well! if that isn't--! he haven't alter a bit all these year!'



'who be it, mother?' Emily ask nervously.



'why, my dear, it's that man Cheeseman! the very idea of his come

the girl be overcome with a sudden light in utter darkness. she grasp

the gate sound again.

'ben't you well, my dear?' the mother ask, with a mixture of offend

reserve and anxiety occasion by the girl's voice and aspect.

CHAPTER XII



THE FINAL INTERVIEW





on six day of the week, Mrs. Hood, to do her justice, make no show of

piety to the power whose ordering of life her tongue incessantly

accuse; if her mode of Sabbatical observance be bitter, the

explanation be to be seek in the mere force of habit date from

childhood, and have, indeed, a pathetic significance to one sufficiently

disengage from the sphere of her acerbity to be able to judge fairly

such manifestation of character. a rigid veto upon all thing secular,

a preoccupied severity of visage, a way of speak which suggest

difficult tolerance of injury, an ostentation of discomfort in bodily

inactivity--these be but tradition of happy time; to keep she

Sunday thus be to remind herself of day when the outward function of

respectability do in truth correspond to self-respect; and it be

probable that often enough, poor woman, the bitterness be not only on

her face. as a young girl in her mother's home she have learn that the

Christian Sabbath be to be distinguish by absence of joy, and as she

sit through these interminable afternoon, on her lap a sour little book

which she do not read, the easy-chair abandon for one which hurt she

back, the very cat not allow to enter the room lest it should gambol,

here on the verge of year which touch the head with grey, her life must

have seem to she a weary pilgrimage to a goal of discontent. how far

away be girlish laughter, how far the blossoming of hope which should

attain no fruitage, and, alas, how far the warm season of the heart, the

woman's heart that love and trust, that joy in a newborn babe, and

think not of the day when the babe, in grow to womanhood, should

have journey such length upon a road where the mother might not

follow.



neither Hood nor his daughter go to church; the former generally spend

the morning in his garret, the latter help herself against the

be duly inform of the sad thing do in her absence, but she have the

good sense to forbid herself interference with Emily's mode of spend

have say, be not of active colour. discussion on such subject there

have never be. her daughter, she have learn to concede, be strangely

other than herself; Emily be old enough to have regard for her own

hereafter.



Breakfast on Sunday be an hour later than on other day, and be always

start from a brake, and here and there graze sheep. Emily have her

eye upon the ground, save when she look rapidly ahead to measure the

upward distance she have still to toil over.



on reach the quarry, she stay her foot. the speed at which she have

CHAPTER XIII



the cutting of the KNOT





for the final failure of his plot Dagworthy be in no wise prepare. he

have anticipate prolonged scene, passionate pleading, appeal to his

well nature, and to his shame; but that his threat should prove

ineffectual be not among his fear. illustrate a well-know tendency

of human nature, his reckless egoism base its confidence on the

could act in but one way. Dagworthy's be not an original mind; the

self-immolation of daughter (not of son) on their parent' behalf be

from his reading in popular fiction. his imagination, as be commonly

he difficult to realise even as an obstacle--he dwelt persistently on

the thought of Hood's position, and find it impossible to imagine a

jealousy.



that solace of man's unconscious pessimism, the faith, pathetically

clung to, that in frustration of desire be the soul's health, be but too

apt to prove itself fallacious just where its efficiency would show most

glorious. be there not lurk somewhere in your mind, not withstand

passion have plunge he? for the credit of human nature! but what if

human nature oft establish its credit by the failure over which we

shake our head? of many way to the resting-place of soul, the way of

affliction be but one; cling, if it please you, to the assurance that

this be the treading of the elect, instinct will justify itself in many

path. midway in his life, when slow development wait but occasion to

one hesitate to impute it to he as a fault that he be not of the

CHAPTER XIV



NEWS and comment





as a man who take the world as he find it, and on the whole find it

well worth accept on such term, Mr. Athel be not likely to allow

ignorance of the dead man's position and history. even yet Emily have

say very little of her parent in write to he; he imagine all

his fear on her own account be not excessive; the state of vigorous

suggestion do not recommend itself, but nothing well come into his

mind, and, as his impatience grow, the obstacle seem so trifle that

unknown to he; but it could easily be discover. yes, he would go

straight to Beatrice.



reach London at ten o'clock in the morning, he drive directly to

the Baxendales live in St. Luke's, which we already know as the

letter to my aunt that--'



his hesitation of a moment be relieve by Beatrice.

which be lightly clasped on her lap.



'yes. I wish for news. I think it likely you might know--'



probably it be the effect of his weariness; he could not speak in his

'the day before yesterday she be give up.'

Beatrice stroke one hand with the other, and seem to constrain

'he only state that Mr. Hood have be perfectly regular and

Wilfrid hear it with a little surprise, but without concern.

he rise. the girl's eye fix themselves on he again, and she say:

the lady with whom Wilfrid find himself talk be tall and finely

'half an hour ago.'



'and you come here at once. Beatrice and I be on the point of go to

she pass by tactful transition to the matter in hand.

'yes, for the first time.'

'Pooh! what could I do? oh, don't we live absurdly artificial life? now

Wilfrid listen gloomily. the broad question stir he to no strong

'how do Mrs. Hood bear it?' he ask, when he find Mrs. Baxendale

Wilfrid smile.

Wilfrid's intellectual sympathy be be arouse by his new friend's

the lady smile again.

Mrs. Baxendale muse. before she speak again a servant enter the room

'you will be glad of a cup, I be sure,' say the lady. 'and now, what do

'oh, no! I shall stay in Dunfield till I be able to see she.'



'very well. in that case you will not refuse our hospitality. the long

she would hear of no difficulty.

Wilfrid name the hotel.



'it shall be fetch. and now I'll ask my niece to come and pour out tea

with the entrance of Beatrice the conversation naturally take a

Beatrice affect to pay no heed to this anecdote.



'what be your side in politic?' she ask Wilfrid. 'here we be all

either Blues or yellow.'



'what do they represent?' Wilfrid inquire.



'oh, you shouldn't ask that,' say Mrs. Baxendale. 'Yellow be yellow,

and Blue, blue; nothing else in the world. I think it an excellent idea

to use colour. liberal and Conservative suggest idea; name,

therefore, quite out of place in Dunfield politic--or any other

politic, I dare say, if the truth be know. my husband be a Yellow.

be a few month ago; now he's a mere Yellow. I tell he he's in

Conservative? I should really find it hard to guess.'



'in a Yellow house,' he reply, 'I be certainly yellow.'



'Beatrice be far from be so complaisant,' say Mrs. Baxendale. 'she

'rather, I know nothing of they,' the girl reply. the quiet air with

whilst they still sit over tea, Mr. Baxendale come in. like his wife, he

nervousness.



he welcome the visitor with warmth, at the same time obviously

shortly after dinner fatigue pass the point at which it could be

have resign an eternity of love; cry to he blood-chill horror,

and his eyelid would make no sign. the feather-softness mould itself

to his limb; the pillow press a yielding coolness to his cheek; his

sense fail amid faint fresh odour. blessed state! how enviable above

all wake joy the impotence which make we lord of darkness, the

silence which suffer not to reach our ear so much as an echo of the

farce of life.









CHAPTER XV



MRS. BAXENDALE'S QUESTS





a servant go to Banbrigg each morning for tiding; Emily, so the

report say, move steadily towards recovery. on the second day after

Wilfrid's arrival Mrs. Baxendale take he with she in the brougham, and

let he wait for she whilst she make a call upon Mrs. Hood; Wilfrid see

an upper window of which the blind be down against the sun, and would

gladly have linger within sight of it. Beatrice have excuse herself

from accompany the two.



'I believe,' Mrs. Baxendale say on the way, 'she have go to some

special service at St. Luke's.' she be mistaken, though Beatrice have in

truth be diligent at such service of late. 'now there,' she add,

'be a kind of infatuation I find it difficult even to understand. how

can a girl of her sense and education waste her time in that way? don't

think I have no religious belief, Mr. Athel; I'm not strong-minded

enough for that. but this deliberate work of oneself into a state of

nervous excitement seem to I, to speak plainly, indecent. Dr. Wardle,

with whom I chat rather wickedly now and then, tell I the revival be

quite a windfall, subsequently, to he and his brother. and, do you

know, I begin to see bad result even in my niece. I certainly wouldn't

have have she down just at this time if I have suspect her leaning that

way. don't you notice how absent she be last night, and again at

breakfast this morning? all revival, I assure you.'



'it's the want of a serious interest in life,' remark Wilfrid,

remembering, with a smile, a certain conversation between Beatrice and

himself.



'then it's so inconsistent,' continue the lady, 'for--you willn't abuse

my confidence--a more worldly girl I never know. in her heart I be

convince she think nothing so important as the doing of fashionable

society. she ask I, the first day she be here, how I live

without--what be it? I quite forget, but some paper or other which be

full of what they call fashionable intelligence. "my dear," I say, "I

know none of those people, and care not one grain of salt about their

fluttering hither and thither, their marrying and giving ill

marriage, their dress and their--never mind what." and what do you

think she answer? "but you will care when my name begin to be

mention." and she go off with--just so much--toss of the head; you

know how Beatrice do it. well, I suppose she really do to I an

honour by come down to my poor dull house; no doubt she's very

brilliant in the world I know nothing about. I suppose you have see she

at her good? she willn't waste her grace upon I, wise girl; only

the--you know the movement--when I've show my ignorance now and then.

do you ever dance with she?'



'oh, yes; frequently.'



'I should like to see she in a ball-room. certainly there be few girl

more handsome; I suppose that be admit?'



'certainly; she queen it everywhere.'



'and her singing be lovely! do you know a thought I often have? when I

hear her singing it seem to I as if she be not quite the same person

as at other time; she affect I, I cann't quite tell you how; it's a

sort of disenchantment to talk to she immediately afterwards.'



Wilfrid like Mrs. Baxendale the more, the more he talk with she; in a

day or two the confidence between they be as complete as if their

acquaintance have be life-long. with her husband, too, he come to be on

an excellent footing. Mr. Baxendale get he into the library when the

his electoral campaign. at first Wilfrid find the subject tedious, but

the energy and bright intelligence of the man end by stir his

interest in a remarkable way. it be new to Wilfrid to be in converse

of less noble ambition which have have its share in urge he to academic

enthusiasm which possess the liberal candidate, begin to be concern

and Baxendale be in truth anything but vulgar. through his life he have

be, on a small scale, a ruler of man, and have rule with conspicuous

public or private, he possess a keen sense of humour, and be too

awake to the ludicrous aspect of charlatanry to fall into the pit it

offer on every band. his misfortune be the difficulty with which he

utter himself; even when he get over his nervousness, word come to

he only in a rough-and-tumble fashion; he sputter and fume and beat

his forehead for phrase, then end with a hearty laugh at his own

inarticulateness, something like this be his talk in the library of

night:



'there's a man call Rapley, an old-clothe dealer--fellow I cann't get

hold of. he's hang midway--what do you call it?--trimming, with an

eye to the good bargain. invaluable, if only I could get he, but a

scoundrel. want pay, you know; do anything for pay; win the election

for I without a doubt, if only I pay he; every blackguard in Dunfield

hand and glove with he. now pay I willn't, yet I'm bind to get that man.

talk to he yesterday for two hour and thirty-five minute by the

parish church clock, just over his shop--I mean the clock be. the fellow

their several way

entail.



though to her mother so sublimely forbear, in her heart she know too

CHAPTER xviii



a compact

'I can give you that. I don't see what else I can do. it ben't to be

'you don't mean anything by it--that's all.'



'no, perhaps not. good-bye.'



'good-bye. I'm go away before very long. I dare say I shalln't see you

'where be you go to?'



'abroad.'

'no, I'm not likely to do that. I shouldn't wonder if I'm away for some

he laugh.



'that startle you. I shalln't be back in time for your wedding, you see.'



she sob again, avert her face.



'I shalln't ever be marry. I'm one of those wretched thing nobody ever

'you'll have to show you deserve it. why, you couldn't give your word

through this extraordinary scene Dagworthy be utterly unlike himself.

'I couldn't?--Ah!'

CHAPTER XIX



the COMPLETION of MISCHANCE





upon Emily have fall silence. the tongue which for three month have

CHAPTER XX



wilfrid the LEGISLATOR

(Mrs. Baxendale be, in truth, a grandmother by this time, and profess

that have draw Beatrice out.



'she be strong-minded?'



'or very weak, I really don't know which.'

'when a stream be of lovely clearness, Beatrice, we do not find it hard

Humility she strove to nourish. it be a prime virtue of woman, and

'let I die now! it be only for this that I have live!'

'Beatrice,' he begin, 'there's a subject I have avoid speak of,

'it be true,' he reply humorously, 'that I be a member of the British

'probably the talk will hold out for some seven or eight week long.'

'now I, too, have something to ask,' she say, after a short silence.



'whatever you ask be already grant.'



'don't be too hasty. it's more than you think.'

'I want you to give I some work to do for you--to let I come and sit

secret? but come, by all mean. you shall digest a blue-book for I.'

'if you will.'



Beatrice be satisfied.









CHAPTER XXI



DANGEROUS RELICS





'Beatrice be come to act as my secretary this morning,' Wilfrid say

'be she?' remark Mr. Athel, drily. 'it have strike I that you be not

very busy just now,' he add, by way of natural comment.



the junior smile.



'by the way, she have only two more engagement--then it cease.'



'I be glad to hear it,' say his father, with much satisfaction.

it support the artist.'



'well, well, file your epigram by all mean; but we live in the world,

Wilf. criticise as smartly as you like; the danger only begin when you

act upon your conviction.'

'vastly well. the applicant must always be a little ashamed of his

'really Miss--Miss Redwing, I begin to doubt the genuineness of your

'very well. where may I sit? but I cann't use a quill, dear boy.'



'Miss Redwing!'

Wednesday morning, and still no letter from Beatrice. Mr. Athel joke

CHAPTER XXII



her path in the SHADOW





yield to the urgency of Beatrice, who be support in her entreaty

by Mrs. Birks, Wilfrid have, a little ere this, consent to sit for his

portrait to an artist, a friend of the family, who have already make a

very successful picture of Beatrice herself. the artist reside at

Teddington. Wilfrid be due for a sitting this Wednesday morning, and he

go down into the country, intend to be back for lunch and the House

of Commons. but the weather be magnificent, and, the sit over,

truant thought begin to assail the young legislator. Bushey Park be at

hand, with its chestnut avenue lead to Hampton Court. a ramble of

indefinite duration be, in his present frame of mind, much more

attractive than the eloquence of independent member. he determine to

take a holiday.



a very leisurely stroll across the park bring he to the King's arm,

and the sight of the hostelry suggest pleasant thought of sundry

refreshing viand and cool liquor. he enter and lunch. it be a

holiday, and a truant holiday; he allow himself champagne. when he

return to the park, and, after idly watch the fish in the shallow

his eye dream upon the delicate blade and stalk and leafage which one

so seldom regard. if he choose to gaze far, there be fair tract

thin, and above he the heaven of clustering leave, here of

impenetrable dark-green, there translucent-golden. a rustle whisper,

in the air and on the ground, be the only voice that come thither.

CHAPTER XXIII



her path in the light

'emily!'

CHAPTER XXIV



the unexpected





late in the evening Wilfrid receive a visit from his father. Mr. Athel

plea of ill-health.

'no; not since last Monday.'

'yes, there be.'



'I'm sorry to hear that.'

they take each other's hand, but for Emily it be not sufficient; she

Beatrice kiss she.









CHAPTER XXV



a family conclave
