CHAPTER XXVI



MID-DAY





once more at the Firs. Wilfrid have decide to make this his abode. it

be near enough to London to allow of his go backwards and forwards

as often as might be necessary; his father's town house offer the

mean of change for Emily, and supply he with a _pie-a-terre_ in

time of session. by limit his attendance at the House as far as

decency would allow, he be able to enjoy with small interruption the

quiet of his home in Surrey, and a grow certainty that the life of

the present Parliament would be short encourage he in look forward

to the day when politic would no long exist for he.



he and Emily establish themselves at the Firs towards the end of

December, having spend a week with Mr. Athel on their return from the

Continent. Emily's health have improve, but there be no likelihood that

she would ever be other than a delicate flower, to be jealously guard

from the sky's ruder breath by he to whom she be a life within life.

ambition as he formerly understand it have no more meaning for Wilfrid;

the fine ardour of his be reject gross nourishment and burn in

altar-flame towards the passion-pale woman whom he after all call

wife. Emily be an unfailing inspiration; by her side the nobler zeal of

his youth renew itself; in the light of her pure soul he see the world

as poetry and strove for that detachment of the intellect which in Emily

be a gift of nature.



she, Emily--Emily Athel, as she joy to write herself--move in her new

sphere like a spirit humble by victory over fate. it be a mild winter;

the Surrey hill be tender against the brief daylight, and garden

breathe the freshness of evergreen. when the sun tremble over the

landscape for a short hour, Emily love to stray as far as that hollow

on the heath where she have sit with Wilfrid year ago, and hear he for

the first time speak freely of his aim and his hope. that spot be

sacred; as she stand there beneath the faint blue of the winter sky, all

the exquisite sadness of life, the memory of those whom death have lead to

his kindly haven, the sorrow of new-bear love, the dear heartache for

woe pass into eternity, touch the deep fountain of her nature

and make dim her eye. she would not have have life other than it be

give to she, for she have learn the secret of infinite passion in the

sunless valley of despair.



she rest. in the last few month she have traverse a whole existence;

repose be needful that she might assimilate all her new experience and

range in due order the gift which joy have lavishly heap upon she. the

sky of the south, the murmur of blue sea on shore of glorious name,

the shrine of Art, the hallowed scene where earth's great have

love and wrought, these be no long a dream with her bodily eye she

have look upon Greece and Italy, and to have do so be a

consecration, it cast a light upon her brow. 'talk to I of Rome;'

those be always her word when Wilfrid come to her side in the

evening. 'talk to I of Rome, as you alone can.' and as Wilfrid recall

their life in the world's holy of holy, she close her eye for the

full rapture of the inner light, and her heart sing praise.



Wilfrid be awe by his blessedness. there be time when he scarcely

dare to take in his own that fine-mould hand which be the symbol of

life make perfect; Emily utter thought which make he fear to profane

her purity by his touch. she realise to the uttermost his ideal of

womanhood, none the less so that it seem no child would be bear of she

to trouble the exclusiveness of their love. he clothe she in queenly

garment and do homage at her foot. her beauty be all for he, for

though Emily could grace any scene she find no pleasure in society, and

the hour of absence from home be to Wilfrid full of anxiety to

return. all their plan be for solitude; life be too short for more

than the inevitable concession to the outside world.



but one morning in February, Emily's eye fall upon an announcement in

the newspaper which excite in she a wish to go up to town. among the

list of singer at a concert to be give that day she have catch the

name of Miss Beatrice Redwing. it be Saturday; Wilfrid have no occasion

for leave home and already they have enjoy in advance the two

unbroken day.



'but I should indeed like to hear she,' Emily say, 'and she seem to

sing so rarely.'



'she have only just return to England,' Wilfrid remark



they have hear of Beatrice having be in Florence a week or two prior

to their own stay there. she be travel with the Baxendales. emily

be anxious to meet she, and Wilfrid have hold out a hope that this might

come about in Italy, but circumstance have prove adverse.



'have you see she?' Emily inquire.



her husband have not. he seem at first a little disinclined to go up

for the concert, but on Emily's become silent he hasten to give a

cheerful acquiescence.



'couldn't we see she to-morrow?' she go on to ask.



'no doubt we can. it's only the facing of my aunt's drawing-room on a

Sunday afternoon.'



'O, surely that be needless, Wilfrid? couldn't we go and see she

quietly? she would be at home in the morning, I should think.'



'I should think so. we'll make inquiry to-night.'



they leave home early in the afternoon and procured ticket on their way

from the station to Mr. Athel's. their arrival be quite unexpected,

they find that Mr. Athel have loft town for a day or two. it be all

that Emily need for the completing of her pleasure; her father-in-law

be scrupulously polite in his behaviour to she, but the politeness fall

a little short as yet of entire ease, and conversation with he involve

effort. she run a risk of let Wilfrid perceive the gladness with

which she discover an empty house; he do, in fact, attribute to its

true cause the light-heartedness she show as they sit together at

dinner, and smile to think that he himself share in the feeling of

relief. there be reason why he could not look forward to the evening

with unalloyed happiness, but the unwonted gaiety which shine on Emily's

face, and give a new melody to her voice, move he to tenderness and

gratitude. he feel that it would be well to listen again to the music of

that strong heart whose pain have be his bliss. he overcome his ignoble

anxiety and go to the concert as to a sacred office.



their seat, owe to lateness in apply for they, be not in the

good part of the hall; immediately behind they be the first row of a

cheap section, and two man of indifferent behaviour be seat there

within ear-shot; they be discuss the various name upon the

programme as if for the enlightenment of their neighbour. when Emily

have be sit for a few minute, she find that it have be unwise to

leave her mantle in the cloak-room; there be a bad draught. Wilfrid

go to recover it. whilst wait, Emily become aware that the man

behind she be talk of Miss Redwing; she listen.



'she's married, I think, eh?' say one.



'be to have be, you mean. why, ben't it you tell I the story? o no,

it be Drummond. Drummond know her people, I think.'



'what story, eh?'



'why, she be to have marry a Member of Parliament; what the deuce be

his name? something that remind I of a race-horse, I remember. be it

Blair? no--Athel! that's the name.'



'why don't it come off, then?'



'oh, the honourable member find somebody he like well.'



it be not the end of the conversation, but just then the conductor rise

in his place and there be 'hush.' Wilfrid return at the same

moment. he notice that Emily shiver as he put the covering on her

shoulder. when he be seat she look at he so strangely that he

ask she in a whisper what be the matter. Emily shake her head and

seem to fix her attention on the music.



Beatrice Redwing be the third singer to come forward. whilst she sing

Emily frequently look at her husband. Wilfrid do not notice it, he

be absorb in listen. towards the end Emily, too, lose thought of

everything save the magic with which the air be charge. there be

vociferous demand for an encore and Beatrice give another song.



when the mid-way interval be reach Emily ask her husband if he

would leave the hall. she give no reason and Wilfrid do not question

she. when they be in the carriage she say the draught have be too

severe. Wilfrid keep silence; he be trouble by inexplicable

misgiving.



servant hasten to light the drawing-room on their arrival early

than be expect. Emily throw off her wrap and seat herself near the

fire.



'do you suffer from the chill?' Wilfrid ask, approach she as if

with diffidence.



she turn her face to he, gaze with the sadness which be so much

more natural to she than the joy of two hour ago.



'it be not the draught that make I come away,' she say with gentle

directness. 'I must tell you what it be, Wilfrid. I cannot keep any of

my thought from you.'



'tell I,' he murmur, stand by she.



she relate the substance of the conversation she have overhear, always

keep her eye on he.



'be it true?'



'it be true, Emily.'



between he and she there could be no paltry embarrassment. a direct

question touch both so deeply could be answer only in one way. if

Emily have suffer from a brief distrust, his look and voice, sorrowful

but frank as though he face Omniscience, restore her courage at once.

there might be grief henceforth, but it be share between they.



he speak on and make all plain. then at the last:



'I feel it to be almost impossible that you should net some day know. I

could not tell you, perhaps on her account as much as on my own. but now

I may say what I have no word for before. she love I, and I believe

that I could return her love. when I meet you, how could I marry she? a

stranger see my conduct--you have hear how. it be you who alone can

judge I.'



'and she come to I in that way,' emily murmur. 'she could not only

lose _you_, but give her hand to the woman who rob she!'



'and take my part with everyone, force herself to show a bright face, do

she good to have it understand that it be she herself who break off the

marriage--all this.'



'dare I go to she, Wilfrid? would it be cruel to go to she? I wish to

speak--oh, not one word that would betray my knowledge, but to say that

I love she. do you think I may go?'



'I cannot advise you, emily. wait until the morning and do then what you

think well.'



she decide to go. Beatrice still live with Mrs. Birks, and it be

probable that she would be alone on Sunday morning. it prove to be so.



Wilfrid wait more than an hour for Emily's return. when at length she

enter to he, he see that there be deep content on her countenance.

Emily embrace her husband and lay her head upon his breast. he could

hear her sigh gently.



'she wish to see you, Wilfrid.'



'she receive you kindly?'



'I will tell you all when I have have time to think of it. but she be

sorry you do not come with I. will you go? she will be alone this

afternoon.'



they hold each other in silence. then Emily, raise an awed face, ask

softly:



'where do she find her strength? be her nature so spotless that

self-sacrifice be her high joy? Wilfrid, I could have ask pardon at

her foot; my heart bleed for she.'



'Dearest, you least of all should wonder at the strength which come of

high motive.'



'oh, but to surrender you to another and to witness that other's

happiness! be not my self-denial perhaps a form of selfishness? I only

shrank from love because I dread the reproach of my own heart; I do

good to no one, be only anxious to save myself. she--I dare not think

of it! my nature be so weak. take your love from I and you take my

life.'



Wilfrid's heart leap with the wild joy of a mountain torrent.



'she will not always be alone,' he say, perhaps with the readiness of

the supremely happy to prophesy smooth thing for all. there come the

answer of gentle reproach:



'after love you, Wilfrid?'



'beautiful, that be how it seem to you. there be second love, often

truer than the first.'



'then the first be not love indeed! if I have never see you again, what

meaning would love have ever have for I apart from your name? I only

dream of it till I know you, then it be love first and last. Wilfrid,

my own, my husband--my love till I die!' ....
